codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="229144" data-framerate="29.97" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d2/Embryo.ogv/Embryo.ogv.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Embryo.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>(Video) Earliest gestation stages and blood circulation of a chicken embryo</div></div></div>
<p>Chicken embryos have long been used as <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a> to study developing embryos. Large numbers of embryos can be provided by commercial chicken farmers who sell fertilized eggs which can be easily opened and used to observe the developing embryo. Equally important, embryologists can carry out experiments on such embryos, close the egg again and study the effect later on. For instance, many important discoveries in the area of <a href="/wiki/Limb_development" title="Limb development">limb development</a> have been made using chicken embryos, such as the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Apical_ectodermal_ridge" title="Apical ectodermal ridge">apical ectodermal ridge</a> (AER) and the <a href="/wiki/Zone_of_polarizing_activity" title="Zone of polarizing activity">zone of polarizing activity</a> (ZPA) by John W. Saunders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, scientists researching the ancestry of birds "turned on" a chicken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recessive_gene" title="Recessive gene">recessive gene</a>, <i>talpid2</i>, and found that the embryo jaws initiated formation of teeth, like those found in ancient bird fossils. John Fallon, the overseer of the project, stated that chickens have "...retained the ability to make teeth, under certain conditions... ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genomic_chicks.jpg"><img alt="Chicks atop a picture of a genetic map of a chicken. The chicken genome has 39 pairs of chromosomes, whereas the human genome contains 23 pairs" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2670" data-file-width="1777" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Genomic_chicks.jpg/220px-Genomic_chicks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Genomic_chicks.jpg/330px-Genomic_chicks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Genomic_chicks.jpg/440px-Genomic_chicks.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genomic_chicks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>G. gallus</i> genome has 39 pairs of chromosomes, whereas the human genome contains 23 pairs</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics_and_genomics">Genetics and genomics</span></h3>
<p>Given its eminent role in farming, meat production, but also research, the house chicken was the first bird genome to be sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> At 1.21 Gb, the chicken genome is considerably smaller than other vertebrate genomes, such as the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> (3 Gb). The final gene set contained 26,640 genes (including noncoding genes and <a href="/wiki/Pseudogene" title="Pseudogene">pseudogenes</a>), with a total of 19,119 protein-coding genes in annotation release 103 (2017), a similar number of protein-coding genes as in the human genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h3>
<p>Populations of chickens from high altitude regions like Tibet have special physiological adaptations that result in a higher hatching rate in low oxygen environments. When eggs are placed in a hypoxic environment, chicken embryos from these populations express much more <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> than embryos from other chicken populations. This hemoglobin also has a greater affinity for oxygen, allowing hemoglobin to bind to oxygen more readily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinopsin" title="Pinopsin">Pinopsins</a> were originally discovered in the chicken <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">pineal gland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nakane-Yoshimura-2019_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nakane-Yoshimura-2019-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunology">Immunology</span></h3>
<p>Although all <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">avians</a> appear to have lost <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TLR9" title="TLR9">TLR9</a>, artificial immunity against bacterial pathogens has been induced in neonatal chicks by Taghavi et al 2008 using tailored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oligodeoxynucleotide" title="Oligodeoxynucleotide">oligodeoxynucleotides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brownlie-Allan-2010_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brownlie-Allan-2010-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_dispersal">Origin and dispersal</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg/250px-Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg/375px-Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg/500px-Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gallus_gallus_male_Kaziranga_0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male red junglefowl.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">Galliformes</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> of bird that chickens belong to, is directly linked to the survival of birds when all other dinosaurs went extinct. Water or ground-dwelling fowl, similar to modern <a href="/wiki/Partridge" title="Partridge">partridges</a>, survived the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> that killed all tree-dwelling birds and dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Some of these evolved into the modern galliformes, of which domesticated chickens are a main model. They are descended primarily from the <a href="/wiki/Red_junglefowl" title="Red junglefowl">red junglefowl</a> (<i>Gallus gallus</i>) and are scientifically classified as the same species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature-60">[59]</a></sup> As such, domesticated chickens can and do freely interbreed with populations of red junglefowl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature-60">[59]</a></sup> Subsequent hybridization of the domestic chicken with <a href="/wiki/Grey_junglefowl" title="Grey junglefowl">grey junglefowl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_junglefowl" title="Sri Lankan junglefowl">Sri Lankan junglefowl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_junglefowl" title="Green junglefowl">green junglefowl</a> occurred;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawal_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawal-61">[60]</a></sup> a gene for yellow skin, for instance, was incorporated into domestic birds through hybridization with the grey junglefowl (<i>G. sonneratii</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> In a study published in 2020, it was found that chickens shared between 71% - 79% of their genome with red junglefowl, with the period of domestication dated to 8,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawal_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawal-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:255px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1577" data-file-width="2545" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg/253px-Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg/380px-Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg/506px-Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg 2x" width="253"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_Junglefowl_hen_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red junglefowl hen in India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestication">Domestication</span></h3>
<p>The traditional view is that chickens were <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">first domesticated</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockfighting" title="Cockfighting">cockfighting</a> in Asia, Africa, and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr.org_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-npr.org-2">[2]</a></sup> In the last decade, there have been a number of genetic studies to clarify the origins. According to one early study, a single domestication event of the red junglefowl in what now is the country of <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> gave rise to the modern chicken with minor transitions separating the modern breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oneMatriarch_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-oneMatriarch-63">[62]</a></sup> The red junglefowl, known as the bamboo fowl in many Southeast Asian languages, is well adapted to take advantage of the <a href="/wiki/Bamboo#Mass_flowering" title="Bamboo">vast quantities of seed</a> produced during the end of the multi-decade bamboo seeding cycle, to boost its own reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> In domesticating the chicken, humans took advantage of this predisposition for prolific reproduction of the red junglefowl when exposed to large amounts of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exactly when and where the chicken was domesticated remains a controversial issue. Genomic studies estimate that the chicken was domesticated 8,000 years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawal_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lawal-61">[60]</a></sup> in Southeast Asia and spread to China and India 2000–3000 years later. Archaeological evidence supports domestic chickens in Southeast Asia well before 6000 BC, China by 6000 BC and India by 2000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawal_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lawal-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> A landmark 2020 Nature study that fully sequenced 863 chickens across the world suggests that all domestic chickens originate from a single domestication event of red junglefowl whose present-day distribution is predominantly in southwestern China, northern Thailand and Myanmar. These domesticated chickens spread across Southeast and South Asia where they interbred with local wild species of junglefowl, forming genetically and geographically distinct groups. Analysis of the most popular commercial breed shows that the White Leghorn breed possesses a mosaic of divergent ancestries inherited from subspecies of red junglefowl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Middle Eastern chicken remains go back to a little earlier than 2000 BC in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>; chickens went southward only in the 1st millennium BC. They reached <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> for purposes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockfighting" title="Cockfighting">cockfighting</a> about 1400 BC, and became widely bred only in <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic</a> Egypt (about 300 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHOF_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHOF-71">[70]</a></sup> Phoenicians spread chickens along the Mediterranean coasts as far as Iberia. During the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> (4th–2nd centuries BC), in the Southern Levant, chickens began to be widely domesticated for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26195775_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26195775-3">[3]</a></sup> This change occurred at least 100 years before domestication of chickens spread to Europe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal">Dispersal</span></h3>
<p>Chickens reached Europe circa 800 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Breeding increased under the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, and was reduced in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHOF_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-CHOF-71">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">Genetic sequencing</a> of chicken bones from archaeological sites in Europe revealed that in the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> chickens became less aggressive and began to lay eggs earlier in the breeding season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Three possible routes of introduction into Africa around the early first millennium AD could have been through the Egyptian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile</a> Valley, the East Africa Roman-Greek or Indian trade, or from Carthage and the Berbers, across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara</a>. The earliest known remains are from Mali, Nubia, East Coast, and South Africa and date back to the middle of the first millennium AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHOF_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-CHOF-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Domestic chicken in the Americas before Western contact is still an ongoing discussion, but blue-egged chickens, found only in the Americas and Asia, suggest an Asian origin for early American chickens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHOF_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-CHOF-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>A lack of data from Thailand, Russia, the Indian subcontinent, Southeast Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa makes it difficult to lay out a clear map of the spread of chickens in these areas; better description and genetic analysis of local breeds threatened by <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> may also help with research into this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHOF_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-CHOF-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span></h4>
<p>An unusual variety of chicken that has its origins in South America is the <a href="/wiki/Araucana" title="Araucana">Araucana</a>, bred in southern <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuche</a> people. Araucanas lay blue-green eggs. Additionally, some Araucanas are tailless, and some have tufts of feathers around their ears. It has long been suggested that they pre-date the arrival of European chickens brought by the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish</a> and are evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> trans-Pacific contacts between Asian or Pacific Oceanic peoples, particularly the Polynesians, and South America. In 2007, an international team of researchers reported the results of their analysis of chicken bones found on the <a href="/wiki/Arauco_Peninsula" title="Arauco Peninsula">Arauco Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zona_Sur" title="Zona Sur">south-central Chile</a>. Radiocarbon dating suggested that the chickens were pre-Columbian, and DNA analysis showed that they were related to prehistoric populations of chickens in Polynesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> These results appeared to confirm that the chickens came from Polynesia and that there were transpacific contacts between Polynesia and South America before Columbus's arrival in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, a later report looking at the same specimens concluded:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>A published, apparently pre-Columbian, Chilean specimen and six pre-European Polynesian specimens also cluster with the same European/Indian subcontinental/Southeast Asian sequences, providing no support for a Polynesian introduction of chickens to South America. In contrast, sequences from two archaeological sites on Easter Island group with an uncommon haplogroup from Indonesia, Japan, and China and may represent a genetic signature of an early Polynesian dispersal. Modeling of the potential marine carbon contribution to the Chilean archaeological specimen casts further doubt on claims for pre-Columbian chickens, and definitive proof will require further analyses of ancient DNA sequences and radiocarbon and stable isotope data from archaeological excavations within both Chile and Polynesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The debate for and against a Polynesian origin for South American chickens continued with this 2014 paper and subsequent responses in <i>PNAS</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomsonLebrasseur2014_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThomsonLebrasseur2014-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_by_humans">Use by humans</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming">Farming</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">Poultry farming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicken_as_food" title="Chicken as food">Chicken as food</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:251px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="698" decoding="async" height="428" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg/249px-BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg/373px-BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg/498px-BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg 2x" width="249"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BatteryChicken5DaysOutOfCage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A former <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery</a> hen, five days after release. Note the pale comb – the comb may be an indicator of health or vigor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_and_Prescott,_(2000)_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_and_Prescott,_(2000)-79">[78]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>More than 50 billion chickens are reared annually as a source of  meat and eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> In the United States alone, more than 8 billion chickens are slaughtered each year for meat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> and more than 300 million chickens are reared for egg production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The vast majority of poultry are raised in <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">factory farms</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Worldwatch_Institute" title="Worldwatch Institute">Worldwatch Institute</a>, 74 percent of the world's poultry meat and 68 percent of eggs are produced this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> An alternative to intensive poultry farming is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-range" title="Free-range">free-range</a> farming.
</p><p>Friction between these two main methods has led to long-term issues of <a href="/wiki/Ethical_consumerism" title="Ethical consumerism">ethical consumerism</a>. Opponents of <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a> argue that it harms the environment, creates human health risks and is inhumane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Advocates of intensive farming say that their highly efficient systems save land and food resources owing to increased productivity, and that the animals are looked after in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State-of-the-art" title="State-of-the-art">state-of-the-art</a> environmentally controlled facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reared_for_meat">Reared for meat</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Broiler" title="Broiler">Broiler</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:259px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florida_chicken_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="437" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Florida_chicken_house.jpg/257px-Florida_chicken_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Florida_chicken_house.jpg/386px-Florida_chicken_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Florida_chicken_house.jpg/514px-Florida_chicken_house.jpg 2x" width="257"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florida_chicken_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A commercial chicken house with open sides raising broiler pullets for meat</div></div></div>
<p>Chickens farmed for meat are called <a href="/wiki/Broiler" title="Broiler">broilers</a>. Chickens will naturally live for six or more years, but broiler breeds typically take less than six weeks to reach slaughter size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Free_range" title="Free range">free range</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_(food)" title="Organic (food)">organic</a> broiler will usually be slaughtered at about 14 weeks of age.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reared_for_eggs">Reared for eggs</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egg_as_food" title="Egg as food">Egg as food</a></div>
<p>Chickens farmed primarily for eggs are called layer hens. In total, the UK alone consumes more than 34 million eggs per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Some hen <a href="/wiki/List_of_chicken_breeds" title="List of chicken breeds">breeds</a> can produce over 300 eggs per year, with "the highest authenticated rate of egg laying being 371 eggs in 364 days".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> After 12 months of laying, the commercial hen's egg-laying ability starts to decline to the point where the flock is commercially unviable.  Hens, particularly from <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cage</a> systems, are sometimes infirm or have lost a significant amount of their feathers, and their life expectancy has been reduced from around seven years to less than two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browne_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-89">[88]</a></sup>  In the UK and Europe, laying hens are then slaughtered and used in processed foods or sold as 'soup hens'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browne_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-89">[88]</a></sup>  In some other countries, flocks are sometimes <a href="/wiki/Forced_molting" title="Forced molting">force moulted</a>, rather than being slaughtered, to re-invigorate egg-laying.  This involves complete withdrawal of food (and sometimes water) for 7–14 days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patwardhan_and_King,_(2011)_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patwardhan_and_King,_(2011)-90">[89]</a></sup> or sufficiently long to cause a body weight loss of 25 to 35%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster,_(2003)_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster,_(2003)-91">[90]</a></sup> or up to 28 days under experimental conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molino_et_al.,_(2009)_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molino_et_al.,_(2009)-92">[91]</a></sup> This stimulates the hen to lose her feathers, but also re-invigorates egg-production.  Some flocks may be force-moulted several times.  In 2003, more than 75% of all flocks were moulted in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yousaf_and_Chaudhry,_(2008)_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yousaf_and_Chaudhry,_(2008)-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_pets">As pets</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster,_Havana,_Cuba.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4368" data-file-width="2912" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster%2C_Havana%2C_Cuba.jpg/220px-A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster%2C_Havana%2C_Cuba.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster%2C_Havana%2C_Cuba.jpg/330px-A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster%2C_Havana%2C_Cuba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster%2C_Havana%2C_Cuba.jpg/440px-A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster%2C_Havana%2C_Cuba.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_95_year_old_woman_with_her_pet_rooster,_Havana,_Cuba.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 95-year-old woman from <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, Cuba, with her pet rooster</div></div></div>
<p>Keeping <b>chickens as pets</b> became increasingly popular in the 2000s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> among <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/urban" title="wikt:urban">urban</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suburban" title="Suburban">suburban</a> residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> Many people obtain chickens for their egg production but often name them and treat them as any other pet like cats or dogs. Chickens provide companionship and have individual personalities. While many do not cuddle much, they will eat from one's hand, jump onto one's lap, respond to and follow their handlers, as well as show affection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chickens are social, inquisitive, intelligent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> birds, and many find their behaviour entertaining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UPC_good_homes_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-UPC_good_homes-99">[98]</a></sup> Certain breeds, such as <a href="/wiki/Silkie" title="Silkie">Silkies</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Bantam_(poultry)" title="Bantam (poultry)">bantam</a> varieties, are generally docile and are often recommended as good pets around children with disabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> Many people feed chickens in part with kitchen food scraps.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg/220px-Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg/330px-Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg/440px-Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Backyard_heritage_chickens_eating_kitchen_food_waste.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Backyard heritage chickens eating kitchen food scraps.</div></div></div><p><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cockfighting">Cockfighting</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cockfight" title="Cockfight">Cockfight</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cocks_Fighting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="616" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Cocks_Fighting.jpg/220px-Cocks_Fighting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Cocks_Fighting.jpg/330px-Cocks_Fighting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Cocks_Fighting.jpg/440px-Cocks_Fighting.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cocks_Fighting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two cocks fighting</div></div></div>
<p>A cockfight is a contest held in a ring called a cockpit between two cocks known as <i>gamecocks.</i> This term, denoting a cock kept for game, sport, pastime or entertainment, appears in 1646,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> after "cock of the game" used by George Wilson in the earliest known book on the secular sport, <i>The Commendation of Cocks and Cock Fighting</i> of 1607. Gamecocks are not typical farm chickens. The cocks are specially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bred" title="Bred">bred</a> and trained for increased stamina and strength. The comb and <a href="/wiki/Wattle_(anatomy)" title="Wattle (anatomy)">wattle</a> are removed from a young gamecock because, if left intact, they would be a disadvantage during a match. This process is called <a href="/wiki/Dubbing_(poultry)" title="Dubbing (poultry)">dubbing</a>. Sometimes the cocks are given drugs to increase their stamina or thicken their blood, which increases their chances of winning. Cockfighting is considered a traditional sporting event by some, and an example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_cruelty" title="Animal cruelty">animal cruelty</a> by others and is therefore outlawed in most countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> Usually wagers are made on the outcome of the match, with the survivor or last bird standing declared winner.
</p><p>Chickens were originally used for cockfighting, a sport where 2 male chickens (cocks) fight each other until one dies or becomes badly injured. Cocks possess congenital aggression toward all other cocks to contest with females. Studies suggest that cockfights have existed even up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> as a pastime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman-104">[103]</a></sup> Today it is commonly associated with religious worship, pastime, and gambling in Asian and some South American countries. While not all fights are to the death, most use metal spurs as a weapon attached above or below the chicken's own spur, which typically results in death in one or both cocks. If chickens are in practice, owners place gloves on the spurs to prevent injuries. Cockfighting has been banned in most western countries and debated by animal rights activists for its brutality.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_incubation">Artificial incubation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:226px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egg_incubator.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="346" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Egg_incubator.jpg/224px-Egg_incubator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Egg_incubator.jpg/336px-Egg_incubator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Egg_incubator.jpg 2x" width="224"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egg_incubator.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An egg incubator</div></div></div>
<p>Incubation can occur artificially in machines that provide the correct, controlled environment for the developing chick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> The average incubation period for chickens is 21 days but the duration depends on the temperature and humidity in the incubator. Temperature regulation is the most critical factor for a successful hatch. Variations of more than 1 °C (1.8 °F) from the optimum temperature of 37.5 °C (99.5 °F) will reduce hatch rates. Humidity is also important because the rate at which eggs lose water by evaporation depends on the ambient relative humidity. Evaporation can be assessed by candling, to view the size of the air sac, or by measuring weight loss. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">Relative humidity</a> should be increased to around 70% in the last three days of incubation to keep the membrane around the hatching chick from drying out after the chick cracks the shell. Lower humidity is usual in the first 18 days to ensure adequate evaporation. The position of the eggs in the incubator can also influence hatch rates. For best results, eggs should be placed with the pointed ends down and turned regularly (at least three times per day) until one to three days before hatching. If the eggs aren't turned, the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> inside may stick to the shell and may hatch with physical defects. Adequate ventilation is necessary to provide the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> with oxygen. Older eggs require increased ventilation.
</p><p>Many commercial incubators are industrial-sized with shelves holding tens of thousands of eggs at a time, with rotation of the eggs a fully automated process. Home incubators are boxes holding from 6 to 75 eggs; they are usually electrically powered, but in the past some were heated with an oil or paraffin lamp.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases_and_ailments">Diseases and ailments</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Poultry_disease" title="Poultry disease">Poultry disease</a></div>
<p>Chickens are susceptible to several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lice" title="Lice">lice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">fleas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roundworm" title="Roundworm">intestinal worms</a>, as well as other diseases.  Despite the name, they are not affected by <a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">chickenpox</a>, which is generally restricted to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chickens can carry and transmit <a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">salmonella</a> in their dander and feces. In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> advise against bringing them indoors or letting small children handle them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the diseases that can affect chickens are shown below:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="569" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg/250px-Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg/375px-Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg/500px-Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_junglefowl_hm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two <a href="/wiki/Red_junglefowl" title="Red junglefowl">red junglefowl</a>, a cock and a hen</div></div></div>
<p>An early domestication of chickens in Southeast Asia is probable, since the word for domestic chicken (<i>*manuk</i>) is part of the reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Proto-Austronesian_language" title="Proto-Austronesian language">Proto-Austronesian language</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033199720" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable crossreference" role="note">(see <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a>)</span>. Chickens, together with dogs and pigs, were the domestic animals of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lapita" title="Lapita">Lapita</a> culture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> the first <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> culture of Oceania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first pictures of chickens in Europe are found on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corinth,_Greece" title="Corinth, Greece">Corinthian</a> <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> of the 7th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chickens were spread by <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> seafarers and reached <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a> in the 12th century AD, where they were the only domestic animal, with the possible exception of the <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_rat" title="Polynesian rat">Polynesian rat</a> (<i>Rattus exulans</i>). They were housed in extremely solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_coop" title="Chicken coop">chicken coops</a> built from stone, which was first reported as such to Linton Palmer in 1868, who also "expressed his doubts about this".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
